     <p>
      <b>
       Magnetic resonance imaging
      </b>
      (
      <b>
       MRI
      </b>
      ),
      <b>
       nuclear magnetic resonance imaging
      </b>
      (
      <b>
       NMRI
      </b>
      ), or
      <b>
       magnetic resonance tomography
      </b>
      (
      <b>
       MRT
      </b>
      ) is a
      <a href="/wiki/Medical_imaging" title="Medical imaging">
       medical imaging
      </a>
      technique used in
      <a href="/wiki/Radiology" title="Radiology">
       radiology
      </a>
      to investigate the
      <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">
       anatomy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">
       physiology
      </a>
      of the body in both health and disease. MRI scanners use
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_fields" title="Magnetic fields">
       magnetic fields
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">
       radio waves
      </a>
      to form images of the body. The technique is widely used in hospitals for medical diagnosis, staging of disease and follow-up without exposure to
      <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">
       ionizing radiation
      </a>
      .
     </p>

             Magnetization transfer MRI
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41">
           <a href="#T1rho_MRI">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             8.2
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">

           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42">
           <a href="#Fluid_attenuated_inversion_recovery_.28FLAIR.29">
            <span class="tocnumber">

            <span class="tocnumber">

          </li>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">
       Introduction
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Introduction">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      MRI has a wide range of applications in medical diagnosis and over 25,000 scanners are estimated to be in use worldwide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      MRI has an impact on diagnosis and treatment in many specialties although the effect on improved health outcomes is uncertain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollingworthTodd2000_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HollingworthTodd2000-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since MRI does not use any ionizing radiation, its use is generally favored in preference to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography" title="X-ray computed tomography">
       CT
      </a>
      when either modality could yield the same information.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iRefer_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-iRefer-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (In certain cases MRI is not preferred as it can be more expensive, time-consuming, and claustrophobia-exacerbating).
     </p>
     <p>
      MRI is in general a safe technique but the number of incidents causing patient harm has risen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Contraindications to MRI include most
      <a href="/wiki/Cochlear_implant" title="Cochlear implant">
       cochlear implants
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Artificial_cardiac_pacemaker" title="Artificial cardiac pacemaker">
       cardiac pacemakers
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(weaponry)" title="Fragmentation (weaponry)">
       shrapnel
      </a>
      and metallic
      <a href="/wiki/Foreign_body" title="Foreign body">
       foreign bodies
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Orbit_(anatomy)" title="Orbit (anatomy)">
       orbits
      </a>
      . The safety of MRI during the first trimester of pregnancy is uncertain, but it may be preferable to alternative options.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WangChong2012_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WangChong2012-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The sustained increase in demand for MRI within the
      <a href="/wiki/Health_care_industry" title="Health care industry">
       healthcare industry
      </a>
      has led to concerns about
      <a href="/wiki/Cost-effectiveness_analysis" title="Cost-effectiveness analysis">
       cost effectiveness
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Overdiagnosis" title="Overdiagnosis">
       overdiagnosis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith-Bindman2012_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith-Bindman2012-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroimaging">
       Neuroimaging
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Neuroimaging">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI_of_brain_and_brain_stem" title="MRI of brain and brain stem">
       MRI of brain and brain stem
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White_Matter_Connections_Obtained_with_MRI_Tractography.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="486" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/White_Matter_Connections_Obtained_with_MRI_Tractography.png/220px-White_Matter_Connections_Obtained_with_MRI_Tractography.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/White_Matter_Connections_Obtained_with_MRI_Tractography.png/330px-White_Matter_Connections_Obtained_with_MRI_Tractography.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/White_Matter_Connections_Obtained_with_MRI_Tractography.png/440px-White_Matter_Connections_Obtained_with_MRI_Tractography.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:White_Matter_Connections_Obtained_with_MRI_Tractography.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        MRI image of white matter tracts.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      MRI is the investigative tool of choice for neurological cancers, as it is more sensitive than CT for small tumors and offers better visualization of the
      <a href="/wiki/Posterior_cranial_fossa" title="Posterior cranial fossa">
       posterior fossa
      </a>
      . The contrast provided between
      <a href="/wiki/Grey_matter" title="Grey matter">
       grey
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">
       white matter
      </a>
      makes it the optimal choice for many conditions of the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
       central nervous system
      </a>
      including
      <a href="/wiki/Demyelinating_disease" title="Demyelinating disease">
       demyelinating diseases
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">
       dementia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebrovascular_disease" title="Cerebrovascular disease">
       cerebrovascular disease
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_infections_of_the_central_nervous_system" title="List of infections of the central nervous system">
       infectious diseases
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">
       epilepsy
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since many images are taken milliseconds apart, it shows how the brain responds to different stimuli; researchers can then study both the functional and structural brain abnormalities in psychological disorders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abnormal_Psychology_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Abnormal_Psychology-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      MRI is also used in MRI-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Image_guided_surgery" title="Image guided surgery">
       guided
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Stereotactic_surgery" title="Stereotactic surgery">
       stereotactic surgery
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Radiosurgery" title="Radiosurgery">
       radiosurgery
      </a>
      for treatment of intracranial tumors, arteriovenous malformations and other surgically treatable conditions using a device known as the
      <a href="/wiki/N-localizer" title="N-localizer">
       N-localizer
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiovascular">
       Cardiovascular
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cardiovascular">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Cardiac magnetic resonance imaging">
       Cardiac magnetic resonance imaging
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PAPVR.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="176" data-file-width="240" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/PAPVR.gif/220px-PAPVR.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/PAPVR.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/PAPVR.gif 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PAPVR.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        MR angiogram in congenital heart disease
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Cardiac MRI is complementary to other imaging techniques, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Echocardiography" title="Echocardiography">
       echocardiography
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Computed_tomography_of_the_heart" title="Computed tomography of the heart">
       cardiac CT
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">
       nuclear medicine
      </a>
      . Its applications include assessment of
      <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">
       myocardial ischemia and viability
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cardiomyopathy" title="Cardiomyopathy">
       cardiomyopathies
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Myocarditis" title="Myocarditis">
       myocarditis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_overload" title="Iron overload">
       iron overload
      </a>
      , vascular diseases and
      <a href="/wiki/Congenital_heart_defect" title="Congenital heart defect">
       congenital heart disease
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Musculoskeletal">
       Musculoskeletal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Musculoskeletal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Applications in the musculoskeletal system include
      <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">
       spinal imaging
      </a>
      , assessment of
      <a href="/wiki/Joint" title="Joint">
       joint
      </a>
      disease and
      <a href="/wiki/Soft_tissue_pathology" title="Soft tissue pathology">
       soft tissue tumors
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Liver_and_gastrointestinal_MRI">
       Liver and gastrointestinal MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Liver and gastrointestinal MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Hepatobiliary_system" title="Hepatobiliary system">
       Hepatobiliary
      </a>
      MR is used to detect and characterize lesions of the
      <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">
       liver
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">
       pancreas
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bile_ducts" title="Bile ducts">
       bile ducts
      </a>
      . Focal or diffuse disorders of the liver may be evaluated using
      <a href="/wiki/Diffusion_MRI" title="Diffusion MRI">
       diffusion-weighted
      </a>
      , opposed-phase imaging and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamic_Contrast_Enhanced_MRI" title="Dynamic Contrast Enhanced MRI">
       dynamic contrast enhancement
      </a>
      sequences. Extracellular contrast agents are widely used in liver MRI and newer hepatobiliary contrast agents also provide the opportunity to perform functional biliary imaging. Anatomical imaging of the bile ducts is achieved by using a heavily T2-weighted sequence in magnetic resonance cholangiopancreatography (MRCP). Functional imaging of the pancreas is performed following administration of
      <a href="/wiki/Secretin" title="Secretin">
       secretin
      </a>
      . MR enterography provides non-invasive assessment of inflammatory bowel disease and small bowel tumors. MR-colonography can play a role in the detection of large polyps in patients at increased risk of colorectal cancer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FrydrychowiczLubner2012_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FrydrychowiczLubner2012-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandrasegaranLin2010_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SandrasegaranLin2010-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MasselliGualdi2012_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MasselliGualdi2012-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZijtaBipat2009_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ZijtaBipat2009-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Functional_MRI">
       Functional MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Functional MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Functional magnetic resonance imaging">
       Functional magnetic resonance imaging
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Functional MRI (fMRI) is used to understand how different parts of the brain respond to external
      <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">
       stimuli
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Blood-oxygen-level_dependent" title="Blood-oxygen-level dependent">
       Blood oxygenation level dependent
      </a>
      (BOLD) fMRI measures the hemodynamic response to transient neural activity resulting from a change in the ratio of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxyhemoglobin" title="Oxyhemoglobin">
       oxyhemoglobin
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deoxyhemoglobin" title="Deoxyhemoglobin">
       deoxyhemoglobin
      </a>
      . Statistical methods are used to construct a 3D
      <a href="/wiki/Statistical_parametric_mapping" title="Statistical parametric mapping">
       parametric map
      </a>
      of the brain indicating those regions of the cortex which demonstrate a significant change in activity in response to the task. FMRI has applications in
      <a href="/wiki/Human_behavior" title="Human behavior">
       behavioral
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">
       cognitive research
      </a>
      as well as in planning
      <a href="/wiki/Neurosurgery" title="Neurosurgery">
       neurosurgery
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Eloquent_cortex" title="Eloquent cortex">
       eloquent brain areas
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GiussaniRoux2010_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GiussaniRoux2010-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeegerRess2002_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HeegerRess2002-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oncology">
       Oncology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Oncology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      MRI is the investigation of choice in the preoperative
      <a href="/wiki/Cancer_staging" title="Cancer staging">
       staging
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">
       rectal
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">
       prostate cancer
      </a>
      , and has a role in the diagnosis, staging, and follow-up of other tumors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="How_MRI_works">
       How MRI works
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: How MRI works">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Physics_of_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Physics of magnetic resonance imaging">
       Physics of magnetic resonance imaging
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MRI-Philips.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2056" data-file-width="2254" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/MRI-Philips.JPG/220px-MRI-Philips.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/MRI-Philips.JPG/330px-MRI-Philips.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/MRI-Philips.JPG/440px-MRI-Philips.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MRI-Philips.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Medical MRI scanner
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      To perform a study, the patient is positioned within an MRI scanner which forms a strong
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">
       magnetic
      </a>
      field around the area to be imaged. In most medical applications,
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       protons
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">
       atoms
      </a>
      ) in tissues containing
      <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">
       water molecules
      </a>
      are used to create a signal that is processed to form an image of the body. First, energy from an
      <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">
       oscillating
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
       magnetic field
      </a>
      is temporarily applied to the patient at the appropriate
      <a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">
       resonance
      </a>
      frequency. The
      <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">
       excited
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">
       atoms
      </a>
      emit a
      <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">
       radio frequency
      </a>
      signal which is measured by a
      <a href="/wiki/Radiofrequency_coil" title="Radiofrequency coil">
       receiving coil
      </a>
      . The radio signal can be made to encode position information by varying the main magnetic field using gradient coils. As these coils are rapidly switched on and off they create the characteristic repetitive noise of an MRI scan. The contrast between different tissues is determined by the rate at which excited atoms return to the
      <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">
       equilibrium state
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Exogeny" title="Exogeny">
       Exogenous
      </a>
      contrast agents may be given
      <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">
       intravenously
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Oral_administration" title="Oral administration">
       orally
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Joint_injection" title="Joint injection">
       intra-articularly
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McRobbie_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-McRobbie-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      MRI requires a
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
       magnetic field
      </a>
      that is both strong and
      <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_(physics)" title="Homogeneity (physics)">
       uniform
      </a>
      . The field strength of the magnet is measured in
      <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
       teslas
      </a>
       and while the majority of systems operate at 1.5T, commercial systems are available between 0.2T7T. Most clinical magnets are
      <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">
       superconducting
      </a>
      which requires liquid
      <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
       helium
      </a>
      . Lower field strengths can be achieved with permanent magnets, which are often used in "open" MRI scanners for
      <a href="/wiki/Claustrophobia" title="Claustrophobia">
       claustrophobic
      </a>
      patients.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Contrast_in_MRI">
       Contrast in MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Contrast in MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TR_TE.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="207" data-file-width="373" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/TR_TE.jpg/220px-TR_TE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/TR_TE.jpg/330px-TR_TE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4d/TR_TE.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TR_TE.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Effects of TR and TE on MR signal.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T1t2PD.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="239" data-file-width="541" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/T1t2PD.jpg/220px-T1t2PD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/T1t2PD.jpg/330px-T1t2PD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/T1t2PD.jpg/440px-T1t2PD.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T1t2PD.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Examples of T1 weighted, T2 weighted and PD weighted MRI scans.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Image contrast may be weighted to demonstrate different anatomical structures or pathologies. Each tissue returns to its equilibrium state after excitation by the independent processes of T1 (spin-lattice) and T2 (spin-spin) relaxation.
     </p>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Spin%E2%80%93lattice_relaxation" title="Spinlattice relaxation">
       Spinlattice relaxation
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      To create a T1-weighted image magnetization is allowed to recover before measuring the MR signal by changing the repetition time (TR). This image weighting is useful for assessing the cerebral cortex, identifying fatty tissue, characterizing focal liver lesions and in general for obtaining morphological information, as well as for post-contrast imaging.
     </p>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-spin_relaxation_time" title="Spin-spin relaxation time">
       Spin-spin relaxation time
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      To create a T2-weighted image magnetization is allowed to decay before measuring the MR signal by changing the echo time (TE). This image weighting is useful for detecting
      <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">
       edema
      </a>
      and inflammation, revealing
      <a href="/wiki/Hyperintensity" title="Hyperintensity">
       white matter lesions
      </a>
      and assessing zonal anatomy in the
      <a href="/wiki/Prostate" title="Prostate">
       prostate
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">
       uterus
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Magnetic resonance imaging was invented by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_C._Lauterbur" title="Paul C. Lauterbur">
       Paul C. Lauterbur
      </a>
      in September 1971; he published the theory behind it in March 1973.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lauterbur_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lauterbur-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The factors leading to image contrast (differences in tissue relaxation time values) had been described nearly 20 years earlier by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Erik_Odeblad&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Erik Odeblad (page does not exist)">
       Erik Odeblad
      </a>
      (physician and scientist).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1950,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin_echoes" title="Spin echoes">
       spin echoes
      </a>
      were first detected by
      <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Hahn" title="Erwin Hahn">
       Erwin Hahn
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hahn_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hahn-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and in 1952,
      <a href="/wiki/Herman_Carr" title="Herman Carr">
       Herman Carr
      </a>
      produced a one-dimensional NMR spectrum as reported in his Harvard PhD thesis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
       Soviet Union
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Vladislav_Ivanov_(physicist)" title="Vladislav Ivanov (physicist)">
       Vladislav Ivanov
      </a>
      filed (in 1960) a document with the USSR State Committee for Inventions and Discovery at Leningrad for a Magnetic Resonance Imaging device,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although this was not approved until the 1970s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Damadian_invention.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="640" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Damadian_invention.jpg/220px-Damadian_invention.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Damadian_invention.jpg/330px-Damadian_invention.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Damadian_invention.jpg/440px-Damadian_invention.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Damadian_invention.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Raymond Damadian's "Apparatus and method for detecting cancer in tissue."
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In a 1971 paper in the journal
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">
        Science
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raymond_Damadian" title="Raymond Damadian">
       Raymond Damadian
      </a>
      , an American physician and professor at the Downstate Medical Center
      <a href="/wiki/State_University_of_New_York" title="State University of New York">
       State University of New York
      </a>
      (SUNY), reported that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">
       tumors
      </a>
      and normal tissue can be distinguished in vivo by nuclear magnetic resonance ("NMR"). He suggested that these differences could be used to diagnose
      <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">
       cancer
      </a>
      , though later research would find that these differences, while real, are too variable for diagnostic purposes. Damadian's initial methods were flawed for practical use,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SydneyMorningHerald_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SydneyMorningHerald-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      relying on a point-by-point scan of the entire body and using relaxation rates, which turned out not to be an effective indicator of cancerous tissue.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wallstjournal_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wallstjournal-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While researching the analytical properties of magnetic resonance, Damadian created a hypothetical magnetic resonance cancer-detecting machine in 1972. He filed the first patent for such a machine, U.S. patent #3,789,832 on March 17, 1972, which was later issued to him on February 5, 1974.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">
       National Science Foundation
      </a>
      notes "The patent included the idea of using NMR to 'scan' the human body to locate cancerous tissue."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, it did not describe a method for generating pictures from such a scan or precisely how such a scan might be done.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latimes2003_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-latimes2003-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armenianreporter_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-armenianreporter-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Meanwhile,
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Lauterbur" title="Paul Lauterbur">
       Paul Lauterbur
      </a>
      at
      <a href="/wiki/Stony_Brook_University" title="Stony Brook University">
       Stony Brook University
      </a>
      expanded on Carr's technique and developed a way to generate the first MRI images, in 2D and 3D, using gradients. In 1973, Lauterbur published the first nuclear magnetic resonance image
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lauterbur_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lauterbur-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Filler2009b_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Filler2009b-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the first cross-sectional image of a living mouse in January 1974.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lauterbur2_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lauterbur2-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the late 1970s,
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_Mansfield" title="Peter Mansfield">
       Peter Mansfield
      </a>
      , a physicist and professor at the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">
       University of Nottingham
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
       England
      </a>
      , developed a mathematical technique that would allow scans to take seconds rather than hours and produce clearer images than Lauterbur had. Damadian, along with Larry Minkoff and Michael Goldsmith, obtained an image of a tumor in the thorax of a mouse in 1976.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-damadian_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-damadian-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They also performed the first MRI body scan of a human being on July 3, 1977,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      studies which they published in 1977.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-damadian_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-damadian-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hinshaw_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hinshaw-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1979, Richard S. Likes filed a patent on k-space *
      <a class="external text" href="http://patft.uspto.gov/netacgi/nph-Parser?Sect1=PTO1&amp;Sect2=HITOFF&amp;d=PALL&amp;p=1&amp;u=%2Fnetahtml%2FPTO%2Fsrchnum.htm&amp;r=1&amp;f=G&amp;l=50&amp;s1=4,307,343.PN.&amp;OS=PN/4,307,343&amp;RS=PN/4,307,343" rel="nofollow">
       4,307,343
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      During the 1970s a team led by
      <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
       Scottish
      </a>
      professor
      <a href="/wiki/John_Mallard" title="John Mallard">
       John Mallard
      </a>
      built the first full body MRI scanner at the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">
       University of Aberdeen
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 28 August 1980 they used this machine to obtain the first clinically useful image of a patient's internal tissues using Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI), which identified a primary tumour in the patient's chest, an abnormal liver, and secondary cancer in his bones.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This machine was later used at
      <a href="/wiki/St_Bartholomew%27s_Hospital" title="St Bartholomew's Hospital">
       St Bartholomew's Hospital
      </a>
      , in London, from 1983 to 1993. Mallard and his team are credited for technological advances that led to the widespread introduction of MRI.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1975, the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_San_Francisco" title="University of California, San Francisco">
       University of California, San Francisco
      </a>
      Radiology Department founded the Radiologic Imaging Laboratory (RIL).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With the support of Pfizer, Diasonics, and later Toshiba America MRI, the lab developed new imaging technology and installed systems in the US and worldwide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1981 RIL researchers, including Leon Kaufman and Lawrence Crooks, published
      <i>
       Nuclear Magnetic Resonance Imaging in Medicine
      </i>
      . In the 1980s the book was considered the definitive introductory textbook to the subject.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1980
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bottomley_(scientist)" title="Paul Bottomley (scientist)">
       Paul Bottomley
      </a>
      joined the GE Research Center in Schenectady, NY, and his team ordered the highest field-strength magnet then available  a 1.5T system  and built the first high-field and overcame problems of coil design, RF penetration and signal-to-noise ratio to build the first whole-body MRI/MRS scanner.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sijbers_snr_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sijbers_snr-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The results translated into the highly successful 1.5T MRI product-line, with over 20,000 systems in use today. In 1982, Bottomley performed the first localized MRS in the human heart and brain. After starting a collaboration on heart applications with Robert Weiss at Johns Hopkins, Bottomley returned to the university in 1994 as Russell Morgan Professor and director of the MR Research Division.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although MRI is most commonly performed at 1.5 T, higher fields such as 3T are gaining more popularity because of their increased sensitivity and resolution. In research laboratories, human studies have been performed at up to 9.4 T
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and animal studies have been performed at up to 21.1T.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="2003_Nobel_Prize">
       2003 Nobel Prize
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2003 Nobel Prize">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Reflecting the fundamental importance and applicability of MRI in medicine,
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Lauterbur" title="Paul Lauterbur">
       Paul Lauterbur
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign">
       University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_Mansfield" title="Peter Mansfield">
       Sir Peter Mansfield
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">
       University of Nottingham
      </a>
      were awarded the 2003
      <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">
       Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine
      </a>
      for their "discoveries concerning magnetic resonance imaging". The Nobel citation acknowledged Lauterbur's insight of using magnetic field gradients to determine spatial localization, a discovery that allowed rapid acquisition of 2D images. Mansfield was credited with introducing the mathematical formalism and developing techniques for efficient gradient utilization and fast imaging. The actual research that won the prize was done almost 30 years before while Paul Lauterbur was a professor in the Department of Chemistry at
      <a href="/wiki/Stony_Brook_University" title="Stony Brook University">
       Stony Brook University
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">
       New York
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lauterbur_25-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-lauterbur-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_of_MRI">
       Safety of MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Safety of MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Implants">
       Implants
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Implants">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:104px;max-width:104px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MR_safe_sign.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="100" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/MR_safe_sign.svg/100px-MR_safe_sign.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/MR_safe_sign.svg/150px-MR_safe_sign.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/MR_safe_sign.svg/200px-MR_safe_sign.svg.png 2x" width="100"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         MR-Safe sign
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MR_conditional_sign.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="100" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/MR_conditional_sign.svg/100px-MR_conditional_sign.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/MR_conditional_sign.svg/150px-MR_conditional_sign.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/MR_conditional_sign.svg/200px-MR_conditional_sign.svg.png 2x" width="100"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         MR-Conditional sign
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MR_unsafe_sign.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="100" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/MR_unsafe_sign.svg/100px-MR_unsafe_sign.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/MR_unsafe_sign.svg/150px-MR_unsafe_sign.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/MR_unsafe_sign.svg/200px-MR_unsafe_sign.svg.png 2x" width="100"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         MR-Unsafe sign
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      All patients are reviewed for contraindications prior to MRI scanning. Medical devices and implants are categorized as MR Safe, MR Conditional or MR Unsafe:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        MR-Safe
       </i>
        The device or implant is completely non-magnetic, non-electrically conductive, and non-RF reactive, eliminating all of the primary potential threats during an MRI procedure.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        MR-Conditional
       </i>
        A device or implant that may contain magnetic, electrically conductive or RF-reactive components that is safe for operations in proximity to the MRI, provided the conditions for safe operation are defined and observed (such as 'tested safe to 1.5 teslas' or 'safe in magnetic fields below 500 gauss in strength').
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        MR-Unsafe
       </i>
        Objects that are significantly ferromagnetic and pose a clear and direct threat to persons and equipment within the magnet room.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The MRI environment may cause harm in patients with MR-Unsafe devices such as
      <a href="/wiki/Cochlear_implant" title="Cochlear implant">
       cochlear implants
      </a>
      and most
      <a href="/wiki/Artificial_cardiac_pacemaker" title="Artificial cardiac pacemaker">
       permanent pacemakers
      </a>
      . Several deaths have been reported in patients with pacemakers who have undergone MRI scanning without appropriate precautions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many implants can be safely scanned if the appropriate conditions are adhered to and these are available online (see
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.MRIsafety.com" rel="nofollow">
       www.MRIsafety.com
      </a>
      ). MR Conditional pacemakers are increasingly available for selected patients.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">
       Ferromagnetic
      </a>
      foreign bodies such as
      <a href="/wiki/Shell_(projectile)" title="Shell (projectile)">
       shell
      </a>
      fragments, or metallic implants such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_prosthetics" title="Surgical prosthetics">
       surgical prostheses
      </a>
      and ferromagnetic
      <a href="/wiki/Aneurysm" title="Aneurysm">
       aneurysm
      </a>
      clips are also potential risks. Interaction of the magnetic and radio frequency fields with such objects can lead to heating or torque of the object during an MRI.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
       Titanium
      </a>
      and its alloys are safe from attraction and torque forces produced by the magnetic field, though there may be some risks associated with
      <a href="/wiki/Lenz%27s_law" title="Lenz's law">
       Lenz effect
      </a>
      forces acting on titanium implants in sensitive areas within the subject, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Stapes" title="Stapes">
       stapes
      </a>
      implants in the inner ear.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Projectile_risk">
       Projectile risk
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Projectile risk">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The very high strength of the magnetic field can cause
      <a href="/wiki/Projectile" title="Projectile">
       projectile
      </a>
      effect (or "missile-effect") accidents, where ferromagnetic objects are attracted to the center of the magnet.
      <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">
       Pennsylvania
      </a>
      reported 27 cases of objects becoming projectiles in the MRI environment between 2004 and 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There have been incidents of injury and death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt-fat_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nyt-fat-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt-acc_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nyt-acc-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In one tragic case, a 6-year-old boy died after an MRI exam, after a metal oxygen tank was pulled across the room and crushed the child's head.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HartwigGiovannetti2009_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HartwigGiovannetti2009-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      To reduce the risk of projectile accidents, ferromagnetic objects and devices are typically prohibited in the proximity of the MRI scanner and patients undergoing MRI examinations are required to remove all metallic objects, often by changing into a gown or
      <a href="/wiki/Scrubs_(clothing)" title="Scrubs (clothing)">
       scrubs
      </a>
      , and ferromagnetic detection devices are used at some sites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACRg_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ACRg-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="EEG_cup_electrodes">
       EEG cup electrodes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: EEG cup electrodes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      EEG (
      <a href="/wiki/Electroencephalography" title="Electroencephalography">
       electroencephalography
      </a>
      ) cup electrodes or are categorized as medical accessories and the same MR Safe, MR Conditional and MR Unsafe terminology applies. With the growth of the use of MR technology, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Food_%26_Drug_Administration" title="U.S. Food &amp; Drug Administration">
       U.S. Food &amp; Drug Administration
      </a>
      [FDA] recognized the need for a consensus on standards of practice, and the FDA sought out
      <a href="/wiki/ASTM_International" title="ASTM International">
       ASTM International
      </a>
      [ASTM] to achieve them. Committee F04
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      of ASTM developed F2503, Standard Practice for Marking Medical Devices and Other Items for Safety in the Magnetic Resonance Environment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Genotoxic_effects">
       Genotoxic effects
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Genotoxic effects">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      There is no proven risk of biological harm from even very powerful static magnetic fields.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartwigetal_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hartwigetal-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genotoxic" title="Genotoxic">
       genotoxic
      </a>
      (i.e., potentially carcinogenic) effects of MRI scanning have been demonstrated in vivo and in vitro,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fiechter_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fiechter-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lee-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simi_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Simi-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suzuki_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Suzuki-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      leading a recent review to recommend "a need for further studies and prudent use in order to avoid unnecessary examinations, according to the
      <a href="/wiki/Precautionary_principle" title="Precautionary principle">
       precautionary principle
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartwigetal_70-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hartwigetal-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a comparison of genotoxic effects of MRI compared with those of CT scans, Knuuti et al. reported that even though the DNA damage detected after MRI was at a level comparable to that produced by scans using ionizing radiation (low-dose coronary CT angiography, nuclear imaging, and X-ray angiography), differences in the mechanism by which this damage takes place suggests that the cancer risk of MRI, if any, is unknown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knuuti_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knuuti-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Peripheral_nerve_stimulation_.28PNS.29">
       Peripheral nerve stimulation (PNS)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Peripheral nerve stimulation (PNS)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The rapid switching on and off of the magnetic field gradients is capable of causing nerve stimulation. Volunteers report a twitching sensation when exposed to rapidly switched fields, particularly in their extremities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The reason the peripheral nerves are stimulated is that the changing field increases with distance from the center of the gradient coils (which more or less coincides with the center of the magnet).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although PNS was not a problem for the slow, weak gradients used in the early days of MRI, the strong, rapidly switched gradients used in techniques such as EPI, fMRI, diffusion MRI, etc. are capable of inducing PNS. American and European regulatory agencies insist that manufacturers stay below specified
      <i>
       d
      </i>
      <b>
       B
      </b>
      /
      <i>
       dt
      </i>
      limits (
      <i>
       d
      </i>
      <b>
       B
      </b>
      /
      <i>
       dt
      </i>
      is the change in magnetic field strength per unit time) or else prove that no PNS is induced for any imaging sequence. As a result of
      <i>
       d
      </i>
      <b>
       B
      </b>
      /
      <i>
       dt
      </i>
      limitation, commercial MRI systems cannot use the full rated power of their gradient amplifiers.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Heating_caused_by_absorption_of_radio_waves">
       Heating caused by absorption of radio waves
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Heating caused by absorption of radio waves">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Every MRI scanner has a powerful radio transmitter to generate the electromagnetic field which excites the spins. If the body absorbs the energy, heating occurs. For this reason, the transmitter rate at which energy is absorbed by the body has to be limited (see
      <a href="/wiki/Specific_absorption_rate" title="Specific absorption rate">
       Specific absorption rate
      </a>
      ). It has been argued (
      <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3445217/" rel="nofollow">
       [5]
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.smithsonianmag.com/smart-news/some-tattoo-inks-can-burn-you-during-mri-180949995/?no-ist" rel="nofollow">
       [6]
      </a>
      ) that tattoos made with iron containing dyes can lead to burns on the subject's body.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustic_noise">
       Acoustic noise
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Acoustic noise">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Switching of
      <a href="#Gradients">
       field gradients
      </a>
      causes a change in the
      <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz force">
       Lorentz force
      </a>
      experienced by the gradient coils, producing minute expansions and contractions of the coil itself. As the switching is typically in the audible frequency range, the resulting vibration produces loud noises (clicking, banging, or beeping). This is most marked with high-field machines
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evolution_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Evolution-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and rapid-imaging techniques in which sound pressure levels can reach 120
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A-weighted" title="A-weighted">
       dB(A)
      </a>
      (equivalent to a jet engine at take-off),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and therefore appropriate ear protection is essential for anyone inside the MRI scanner room during the examination.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oucardio_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-oucardio-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cryogens">
       Cryogens
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cryogens">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      As described in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physics_of_Magnetic_Resonance_Imaging#Scanner_construction_and_operation" title="Physics of Magnetic Resonance Imaging">
       Physics of Magnetic Resonance Imaging
      </a>
      , many MRI scanners rely on cryogenic liquids to enable the superconducting capabilities of the electromagnetic coils within. Though the cryogenic liquids used are non-toxic, their physical properties present specific hazards.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An unintentional shut-down of a
      <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnet" title="Superconducting magnet">
       superconducting electromagnet
      </a>
      , an event known as "quench", involves the rapid boiling of liquid
      <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
       helium
      </a>
      from the device. If the rapidly expanding helium cannot be dissipated through an external vent, sometimes referred to as a 'quench pipe', it may be released into the scanner room where it may cause displacement of the oxygen and present a risk of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asphyxiation" title="Asphyxiation">
       asphyxiation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17515363_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17515363-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Oxygen deficiency monitors are usually used as a safety precaution. Liquid
      <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
       helium
      </a>
      , the most commonly used cryogen in MRI, undergoes near explosive expansion as it changes from a liquid to gaseous state. The use of an oxygen monitor is important to ensure that oxygen levels are safe for patient/physicians. Rooms built for superconducting MRI equipment should be equipped with pressure relief mechanisms
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and an exhaust fan, in addition to the required quench pipe.
     </p>
     <p>
      Because a quench results in rapid loss of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogen" title="Cryogen">
       cryogens
      </a>
      from the magnet, recommissioning the magnet is expensive and time-consuming. Spontaneous quenches are uncommon, but a quench may also be triggered by an equipment malfunction, an improper cryogen fill technique, contaminants inside the cryostat, or extreme magnetic or vibrational disturbances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pregnancy">
       Pregnancy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Pregnancy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      No effects of MRI on the fetus have been demonstrated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In particular, MRI avoids the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">
       ionizing radiation
      </a>
      , to which the fetus is particularly sensitive. However, as a precaution, current guidelines recommend that pregnant women undergo MRI only when essential. This is particularly the case during the first trimester of pregnancy, as
      <a href="/wiki/Organogenesis" title="Organogenesis">
       organogenesis
      </a>
      takes place during this period. The concerns in pregnancy are the same as for MRI in general, but the fetus may be more sensitive to the effectsparticularly to heating and to noise. The use of
      <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">
       gadolinium
      </a>
      -based contrast media in pregnancy is an
      <a href="/wiki/Off-label_use" title="Off-label use">
       off-label indication
      </a>
      and may only be administered in the lowest dose required to provide essential diagnostic information.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WebbThomsen2013_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WebbThomsen2013-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite these concerns, MRI is rapidly growing in importance as a way of diagnosing and monitoring
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital_defect" title="Congenital defect">
       congenital defects
      </a>
      of the fetus because it can provide more diagnostic information than
      <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">
       ultrasound
      </a>
      and it lacks the ionizing radiation of CT. MRI without contrast agents is the imaging mode of choice for pre-surgical, in-utero diagnosis and evaluation of
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fetal_tumor&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fetal tumor (page does not exist)">
       fetal tumors
      </a>
      , primarily
      <a href="/wiki/Teratoma" title="Teratoma">
       teratomas
      </a>
      , facilitating open
      <a href="/wiki/Fetal_surgery" title="Fetal surgery">
       fetal surgery
      </a>
      , other
      <a href="/wiki/Fetal_intervention" title="Fetal intervention">
       fetal interventions
      </a>
      , and planning for procedures (such as the
      <a href="/wiki/EXIT_procedure" title="EXIT procedure">
       EXIT procedure
      </a>
      ) to safely deliver and treat babies whose defects would otherwise be fatal.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Claustrophobia_and_discomfort">
       Claustrophobia and discomfort
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Claustrophobia and discomfort">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Although painless, MRI scans can be unpleasant for those who are claustrophobic or otherwise uncomfortable with the imaging device surrounding them. Older closed bore MRI systems have a fairly long tube or tunnel. The part of the body being imaged must lie at the center of the magnet, which is at the absolute center of the tunnel. Because scan times on these older scanners may be long (occasionally up to 40 minutes for the entire procedure), people with even mild claustrophobia are sometimes unable to tolerate an MRI scan without management. Some modern scanners have larger bores (up to 70cm) and scan times are shorter. A 1.5 T wide short bore scanner increases the examination success rate in patients with claustrophobia and substantially reduces the need for anesthesia-assisted MRI examinations even when claustrophobia is severe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuntWood2011_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HuntWood2011-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Alternative scanner designs, such as open or upright systems, can also be helpful where these are available. Though open scanners have increased in popularity, they produce inferior scan quality because they operate at lower magnetic fields than closed scanners. However, commercial 1.5
      <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
       tesla
      </a>
      open systems have recently become available, providing much better image quality than previous lower field strength open models.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Mirror glasses can be used to help create the illusion of openness. The mirrors are angled at 45 degrees, allowing the patient to look down their body and out the end of the imaging area. The appearance is of an open tube pointing upwards (as seen when lying in the imaging area). Even though one can see around the glasses and the proximity of the device is very evident, this illusion is quite persuasive and relieves the claustrophobic feeling.
     </p>
     <p>
