      For babies and other young children, chemical sedation or general anesthesia are the norm, as these subjects cannot be expected or instructed to hold still during the scanning session. Children are also frequently sedated because they are frightened by the unfamiliar procedure and the loud noises. To reduce anxiety, some hospitals have specially designed child-friendly approaches that pretend the MRI machine is a spaceship or other fun experience.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Obese patients and pregnant women may find the MRI machine to be a tight fit. Pregnant women in the third trimester may also have difficulty lying on their backs for an hour or more without moving.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="MRI_versus_CT">
       MRI versus CT
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: MRI versus CT">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      MRI and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computed_tomography" title="Computed tomography">
       computed tomography
      </a>
      (CT) are complementary imaging technologies and each has advantages and limitations for particular applications. CT is more widely used than MRI in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">
       OECD
      </a>
      countries with a mean of 132 vs 46 exams per 1000 population performed respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A concern is the potential for CT to contribute to
      <a href="/wiki/Radiation-induced_cancer" title="Radiation-induced cancer">
       radiation-induced cancer
      </a>
      and in 2007 it was estimated that 0.4% of current cancers in the United States were due to CTs performed in the past, and that in the future this figure may rise to 1.52% based on historical rates of CT usage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brenner2007_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brenner2007-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An Australian study found that one in every 1800 CT scans was associated with an excess cancer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MathewsForsythe2013_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MathewsForsythe2013-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An advantage of MRI is that no ionizing radiation is used and so it is recommended over CT when either approach could yield the same diagnostic information.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iRefer_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-iRefer-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, although the cost of MRI has fallen, making it more competitive with CT, there are not many common imaging scenarios in which MRI can simply replace CT, although this substitution has been suggested for the imaging of liver disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SemelkaArmao2007_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SemelkaArmao2007-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The effect of low doses of radiation on carcinogenesis are also disputed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although MRI is associated with biological effects, these have not been proven to cause measurable harm.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FormicaSilvestri2004_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FormicaSilvestri2004-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a comparison of possible
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genotoxic" title="Genotoxic">
       genotoxic
      </a>
      effects of MRI compared with those of CT scans, Knuuti et al. noted that although previous studies have demonstrated
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_damage" title="DNA damage">
       DNA damage
      </a>
      associated with MRI, "the long-term biological and clinical significance of DNA
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double-strand_breaks#Double-strand_breaks" title="Double-strand breaks">
       double-strand breaks
      </a>
      induced by MRI remains unknown".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knuuti_75-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knuuti-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Radiocontrast_agent" title="Radiocontrast agent">
       Iodinated contrast medium
      </a>
      is routinely used in CT and the main adverse events are anaphylactoid reactions and
      <a href="/wiki/Nephrotoxicity" title="Nephrotoxicity">
       nephrotoxicity
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15486247_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15486247-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Commonly used
      <a href="/wiki/MRI_contrast_agent" title="MRI contrast agent">
       MRI contrast agents
      </a>
      have a good safety profile but linear non-ionic agents in particular have been implicated in
      <a href="/wiki/Nephrogenic_systemic_fibrosis" title="Nephrogenic systemic fibrosis">
       nephrogenic systemic fibrosis
      </a>
      in patients with severely impaired renal function.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACR-NSF_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ACR-NSF-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      MRI is contraindicated in the presence of MR-unsafe implants, and although these patients may be imaged with CT,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography#Image_quality" title="X-ray computed tomography">
       beam hardening
      </a>
      artefact from metallic devices, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Artificial_cardiac_pacemaker" title="Artificial cardiac pacemaker">
       pacemakers
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Implantable_cardioverter-defibrillator" title="Implantable cardioverter-defibrillator">
       implantable cardioverter-defibrillators
      </a>
      , may also affect image quality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MakTruong2012_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MakTruong2012-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      MRI is a longer investigation than CT and an exam may take between 20 - 40 mins depending on complexity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Guidance">
       Guidance
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Guidance">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Safety issues, including the potential for biostimulation device interference, movement of ferromagnetic bodies, and incidental localized heating, have been addressed in the
      <a href="/wiki/American_College_of_Radiology" title="American College of Radiology">
       American College of Radiology
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       White Paper on MR Safety,
      </i>
      which was originally published in 2002 and expanded in 2004. The
      <i>
       ACR White Paper on MR Safety
      </i>
      has been rewritten and was released early in 2007 under the new title
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.acr.org/SecondaryMainMenuCategories/quality_safety/MRSafety/safe_mr07.aspx" rel="nofollow">
       <i>
        ACR Guidance Document for Safe MR Practices
       </i>
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In December 2007, the
      <a href="/wiki/Medicines_and_Healthcare_Products_Regulatory_Agency" title="Medicines and Healthcare Products Regulatory Agency">
       Medicines and Healthcare Products Regulatory Agency
      </a>
      (MHRA), a UK healthcare regulatory body, issued their
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.mhra.gov.uk/home/idcplg?IdcService=GET_FILE&amp;dDocName=CON2033065&amp;RevisionSelectionMethod=LatestReleased" rel="nofollow">
       <i>
        Safety Guidelines for Magnetic Resonance Imaging Equipment in Clinical Use
       </i>
      </a>
      . In February 2008, the
      <a href="/wiki/Joint_Commission" title="Joint Commission">
       Joint Commission
      </a>
      , a US healthcare accrediting organization, issued a
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.jointcommission.org/SentinelEvents/SentinelEventAlert/sea_38.htm" rel="nofollow">
       Sentinel Event Alert #38
      </a>
      , their highest patient safety advisory, on MRI safety issues. In July 2008, the United States Veterans Administration, a federal governmental agency serving the healthcare needs of former military personnel, issued a substantial revision to their
      <i>
       MRI Design Guide
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which includes physical and facility safety considerations.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_European_Directive_on_Electromagnetic_Fields">
       The European Directive on Electromagnetic Fields
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: The European Directive on Electromagnetic Fields">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      This Directive (2013/35/EU - electromagnetic fields)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      covers all known direct biophysical effects and indirect effects caused by electromagnetic fields within the EU and repealed the 2004/40/EC directive. The deadline for implementation of the new directive is 1 July 2016. Article 10 of the directive sets out the scope of the derogation for MRI, stating that the exposure limits may be exceeded during "the installation, testing, use, development, maintenance of or research related to magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) equipment for patients in the health sector, provided that certain conditions are met." Uncertainties remain regarding the scope and conditions of this derogation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KeevilLomas2013_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-KeevilLomas2013-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Contrast_agents">
       Contrast agents
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Contrast agents">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/MRI_contrast_agent" title="MRI contrast agent">
       MRI contrast agent
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The most commonly used intravenous contrast agents are based on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chelate" title="Chelate">
       chelates
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">
       gadolinium
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In general, these agents have proved safer than the iodinated contrast agents used in X-ray radiography or CT.
      <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">
       Anaphylactoid reactions
      </a>
      are rare, occurring in approx. 0.030.1%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Of particular interest is the lower incidence of nephrotoxicity, compared with iodinated agents, when given at usual dosesthis has made contrast-enhanced MRI scanning an option for patients with renal impairment, who would otherwise not be able to undergo
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiocontrast" title="Radiocontrast">
       contrast-enhanced CT
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although gadolinium agents have proved useful for patients with renal impairment, in patients with severe renal failure requiring dialysis there is a risk of a rare but serious illness,
      <a href="/wiki/Nephrogenic_systemic_fibrosis" title="Nephrogenic systemic fibrosis">
       nephrogenic systemic fibrosis
      </a>
      , which may be linked to the use of certain gadolinium-containing agents. The most frequently linked is gadodiamide, but other agents have been linked too.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although a causal link has not been definitively established, current guidelines in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      are that dialysis patients should only receive gadolinium agents where essential, and that
      <a href="/wiki/Dialysis" title="Dialysis">
       dialysis
      </a>
      should be performed as soon as possible after the scan to remove the agent from the body promptly.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Europe, where more gadolinium-containing agents are available, a classification of agents according to potential risks has been released.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recently, a new contrast agent named
      <a href="/wiki/Gadoxetic_acid" title="Gadoxetic acid">
       gadoxetate
      </a>
      , brand name Eovist (US) or Primovist (EU), was approved for diagnostic use: this has the theoretical benefit of a dual excretion path.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Health_economics">
       Health economics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Health economics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In the UK, the price of a clinical 1.5 tesla MRI scanner is around 1,04 million/
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$1.4 million
      </span>
      with the lifetime maintenance cost broadly similar to the purchase cost.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the Netherlands, the average MRI scanner costs around 1 million,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with a 7T MRI having been taken in use by the UMC Utrecht in December 2007, costing 7 million.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Construction of MRI suites could cost up to
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$500,000
      </span>
      /370.000 or more, depending on project scope. Pre-polarizing MRI (PMRI) systems using resistive electromagnets have shown promise as a low cost alternative and have specific advantages for joint imaging near metal implants, however they are unlikely to be suitable for routine whole-body or neuroimaging applications.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorganConolly1996_117-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MorganConolly1996-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blamire2008_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Blamire2008-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2009_niams_oai_hi.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="640" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/2009_niams_oai_hi.jpg/220px-2009_niams_oai_hi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/2009_niams_oai_hi.jpg/330px-2009_niams_oai_hi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/2009_niams_oai_hi.jpg/440px-2009_niams_oai_hi.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2009_niams_oai_hi.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Looking through an MRI scanner.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      MRI scanners have become significant sources of revenue for healthcare providers in the US. This is because of favorable reimbursement rates from insurers and federal government programs. Insurance reimbursement is provided in two components, an equipment charge for the actual performance and operation of the MRI scan and a professional charge for the radiologist's review of the images and/or data. In the US Northeast, an equipment charge might be $3,500/2.600 and a professional charge might be $350/260,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although the actual fees received by the equipment owner and interpreting physician are often significantly less and depend on the rates negotiated with insurance companies or determined by the Medicare fee schedule. For example, an orthopedic surgery group in Illinois billed a charge of $1,116/825 for a knee MRI in 2007, but the Medicare reimbursement in 2007 was only $470.91/350.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many insurance companies require advance approval of an MRI procedure as a condition for coverage.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the US, the
      <a href="/wiki/Deficit_Reduction_Act_of_2005" title="Deficit Reduction Act of 2005">
       Deficit Reduction Act of 2005
      </a>
      significantly reduced reimbursement rates paid by federal insurance programs for the equipment component of many scans, shifting the economic landscape. Many private insurers have followed suit.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the United States, an MRI of the brain with and without contrast billed to Medicare Part B entails, on average, a technical payment of
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$403
      </span>
      /300 and a separate payment to the radiologist of
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$93
      </span>
      /70.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In France, the cost of an MRI exam is approximately 150/
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$205
      </span>
      . This covers three basic scans including one with an intravenous contrast agent as well as a consultation with the technician and a written report to the patient's physician.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      In Japan, the cost of an MRI examination (excluding the cost of contrast material and films) ranges from
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$155
      </span>
      /115 to
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$180
      </span>
      /133, with an additional radiologist professional fee of
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$17
      </span>
      /12,50.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In India, the cost of an MRI examination including the fee for the radiologist's opinion comes to around Rs 30004000 (37-49/
      <span style="white-space: nowrap">
       US$5060
      </span>
      ), excluding the cost of contrast material. In the UK the retail price for an MRI scan privately ranges between 350 and 500 (440-630).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Overuse">
       Overuse
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Overuse">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Medical societies issue guidelines for when physicians should use MRI on patients and recommend against overuse. MRI can detect health problems or confirm a diagnosis, but medical societies often recommend that MRI not be the first procedure for creating a plan to diagnose or manage a patient's complaint. A common case is to use MRI to seek a cause of
      <a href="/wiki/Low_back_pain" title="Low back pain">
       low back pain
      </a>
      ; the
      <a href="/wiki/American_College_of_Physicians" title="American College of Physicians">
       American College of Physicians
      </a>
      , for example, recommends against this procedure as unlikely to result in a positive outcome for the patient.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACPfive_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ACPfive-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-backimage_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-backimage-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Specialized_applications">
       Specialized applications
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Specialized applications">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diffusion_MRI">
       Diffusion MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Diffusion MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Diffusion_MRI" title="Diffusion MRI">
       Diffusion MRI
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:144px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illus_dti.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="198" data-file-width="142" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Illus_dti.gif" width="142"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illus_dti.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        DTI image
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Diffusion_MRI" title="Diffusion MRI">
       Diffusion MRI
      </a>
      measures the
      <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">
       diffusion
      </a>
      of water molecules in biological tissues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Clinically, diffusion MRI is useful for the diagnoses of conditions (e.g.,
      <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">
       stroke
      </a>
      ) or neurological disorders (e.g.,
      <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">
       multiple sclerosis
      </a>
      ), and helps better understand the connectivity of white matter axons in the central nervous system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">
       isotropic
      </a>
      medium (inside a glass of water for example), water molecules naturally move randomly according to
      <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">
       turbulence
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">
       Brownian motion
      </a>
      . In biological tissues however, where the
      <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_number" title="Reynolds number">
       Reynolds number
      </a>
      is low enough for flows to be
      <a href="/wiki/Laminar_flow" title="Laminar flow">
       laminar
      </a>
      , the diffusion may be
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anisotropic" title="Anisotropic">
       anisotropic
      </a>
      . For example, a molecule inside the
      <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">
       axon
      </a>
      of a neuron has a low probability of crossing the
      <a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">
       myelin
      </a>
      membrane. Therefore the molecule moves principally along the axis of the neural fiber. If it is known that molecules in a particular
      <a href="/wiki/Voxel" title="Voxel">
       voxel
      </a>
      diffuse principally in one direction, the assumption can be made that the majority of the fibers in this area are parallel to that direction.
     </p>
     <p>
      The recent development of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diffusion_tensor_imaging" title="Diffusion tensor imaging">
       diffusion tensor imaging
      </a>
      (DTI)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      enables diffusion to be measured in multiple directions and the fractional anisotropy in each direction to be calculated for each voxel. This enables researchers to make brain maps of fiber directions to examine the connectivity of different regions in the brain (using
      <a href="/wiki/Tractography" title="Tractography">
       tractography
      </a>
      ) or to examine areas of neural degeneration and demyelination in diseases like multiple sclerosis.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another application of diffusion MRI is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diffusion-weighted_imaging" title="Diffusion-weighted imaging">
       diffusion-weighted imaging
      </a>
      (DWI). Following an ischemic
      <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">
       stroke
      </a>
      , DWI is highly sensitive to the changes occurring in the lesion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is speculated that increases in restriction (barriers) to water diffusion, as a result of cytotoxic edema (cellular swelling), is responsible for the increase in signal on a DWI scan. The DWI enhancement appears within 510 minutes of the onset of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stroke_symptoms" title="Stroke symptoms">
       stroke symptoms
      </a>
      (as compared to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computed_tomography" title="Computed tomography">
       computed tomography
      </a>
      , which often does not detect changes of acute infarct for up to 46 hours) and remains for up to two weeks. Coupled with imaging of
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.trauma.org/archive/neuro/cpp.html" rel="nofollow">
       cerebral perfusion
      </a>
      , researchers can highlight regions of "perfusion/diffusion mismatch" that may indicate regions capable of salvage by reperfusion therapy.
     </p>
     <p>
      Like many other specialized applications, this technique is usually coupled with a fast image acquisition sequence, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echo_planar_imaging" title="Echo planar imaging">
       echo planar imaging
      </a>
      sequence.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_resonance_angiography">
       Magnetic resonance angiography
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Magnetic resonance angiography">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mra1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="514" data-file-width="514" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Mra1.jpg/220px-Mra1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Mra1.jpg/330px-Mra1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Mra1.jpg/440px-Mra1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mra1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Magnetic resonance angiography
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_angiography" title="Magnetic resonance angiography">
       Magnetic resonance angiography
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Magnetic resonance
      <a href="/wiki/Angiography" title="Angiography">
       angiography
      </a>
      (MRA) generates pictures of the arteries to evaluate them for
      <a href="/wiki/Stenosis" title="Stenosis">
       stenosis
      </a>
      (abnormal narrowing) or
      <a href="/wiki/Aneurysm" title="Aneurysm">
       aneurysms
      </a>
      (vessel wall dilatations, at risk of rupture). MRA is often used to evaluate the arteries of the neck and brain, the thoracic and abdominal aorta, the renal arteries, and the legs (called a "run-off"). A variety of techniques can be used to generate the pictures, such as administration of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" title="Paramagnetic">
       paramagnetic
      </a>
      contrast agent (
      <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">
       gadolinium
      </a>
      ) or using a technique known as "flow-related enhancement" (e.g., 2D and 3D time-of-flight sequences), where most of the signal on an image is due to blood that recently moved into that plane, see also
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FLASH_MRI" title="FLASH MRI">
       FLASH MRI
      </a>
      . Techniques involving phase accumulation (known as phase contrast angiography) can also be used to generate flow velocity maps easily and accurately. Magnetic resonance venography (MRV) is a similar procedure that is used to image veins. In this method, the tissue is now excited inferiorly, while the signal is gathered in the plane immediately superior to the excitation planethus imaging the venous blood that recently moved from the excited plane.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haacke_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-haacke-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy">
       Magnetic resonance spectroscopy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Magnetic resonance spectroscopy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/In_vivo_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="In vivo magnetic resonance spectroscopy">
       In vivo magnetic resonance spectroscopy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy">
       Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/In_vivo_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="In vivo magnetic resonance spectroscopy">
       Magnetic resonance spectroscopy
      </a>
      (MRS) is used to measure the levels of different
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolites" title="Metabolites">
       metabolites
      </a>
      in body tissues. The MR signal produces a spectrum of resonances that corresponds to different molecular arrangements of the isotope being "excited". This signature is used to diagnose certain metabolic disorders, especially those affecting the brain,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and to provide information on tumor
      <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">
       metabolism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Magnetic resonance spectroscopic imaging (MRSI) combines both spectroscopic and imaging methods to produce spatially localized spectra from within the sample or patient. The spatial resolution is much lower (limited by the available
      <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">
       SNR
      </a>
      ), but the spectra in each voxel contains information about many metabolites. Because the available signal is used to encode spatial and spectral information, MRSI requires high SNR achievable only at higher field strengths (3 T and above).
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Functional_MRI_2">
       Functional MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Functional MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Functional magnetic resonance imaging">
       Functional magnetic resonance imaging
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FMRI.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="208" data-file-width="250" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/FMRI.jpg/220px-FMRI.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/15/FMRI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/15/FMRI.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FMRI.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A fMRI scan showing regions of activation in orange, including the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_visual_cortex" title="Primary visual cortex">
         primary visual cortex
        </a>
        (V1, BA17).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Functional_MRI" title="Functional MRI">
       Functional MRI
      </a>
      (fMRI) measures signal changes in the
      <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">
       brain
      </a>
      that are due to changing
      <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">
       neural
      </a>
      activity. Compared to anatomical T1W imaging, the brain is scanned at lower spatial resolution but at a higher temporal resolution (typically once every 23 seconds). Increases in neural activity cause changes in the MR signal via
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
       *
       <br/>
       2
      </span>
      changes;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      this mechanism is referred to as the BOLD (
      <a href="/wiki/Blood-oxygen-level_dependent" title="Blood-oxygen-level dependent">
       blood-oxygen-level dependent
      </a>
      ) effect. Increased neural activity causes an increased demand for oxygen, and the
      <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">
       vascular
      </a>
      system actually overcompensates for this, increasing the amount of oxygenated
      <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">
       hemoglobin
      </a>
      relative to deoxygenated hemoglobin. Because deoxygenated hemoglobin attenuates the MR signal, the vascular response leads to a signal increase that is related to the neural activity. The precise nature of the relationship between neural activity and the BOLD signal is a subject of current research. The BOLD effect also allows for the generation of high resolution 3D maps of the venous vasculature within neural tissue.
     </p>
     <p>
      While BOLD signal analysis is the most common method employed for neuroscience studies in human subjects, the flexible nature of MR imaging provides means to sensitize the signal to other aspects of the blood supply. Alternative techniques employ
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_spin_labeling" title="Arterial spin labeling">
       arterial spin labeling
      </a>
      (ASL) or weighting the MRI signal by cerebral blood flow (CBF) and cerebral blood volume (CBV). The CBV method requires injection of a class of MRI contrast agents that are now in human clinical trials. Because this method has been shown to be far more sensitive than the BOLD technique in preclinical studies, it may potentially expand the role of fMRI in clinical applications. The CBF method provides more quantitative information than the BOLD signal, albeit at a significant loss of detection sensitivity.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Real-time_MRI">
       Real-time MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Real-time MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Real-time_MRI_-_Thorax.ogv" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Real-time MRI of a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_heart" title="Human heart">
         human heart
        </a>
        at a resolution of 50 ms
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Real-time_MRI" title="Real-time MRI">
       Real-time MRI
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Real-time MRI refers to the continuous monitoring ("filming") of moving objects in real time. While many different strategies have been developed over the past two decades, a recent development reported a real-time MRI technique based on radial
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FLASH_MRI" title="FLASH MRI">
       FLASH
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Iterative_reconstruction" title="Iterative reconstruction">
       iterative reconstruction
      </a>
      that yields a temporal resolution of 20 to 30 milliseconds for images with an in-plane resolution of 1.5 to 2.0mm. The new method promises to add important information about diseases of the joints and the heart. In many cases MRI examinations may become easier and more comfortable for patients.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ilyas_Uyanik_2013_133-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ilyas_Uyanik_2013-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Interventional_MRI">
       Interventional MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Interventional MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interventional_MRI" title="Interventional MRI">
       Interventional MRI
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The lack of harmful effects on the patient and the operator make MRI well-suited for "
      <a href="/wiki/Interventional_radiology" title="Interventional radiology">
       interventional radiology
      </a>
      ", where the images produced by an MRI scanner are used to guide minimally invasive procedures. Of course, such procedures must be done without
      <i>
       any
      </i>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">
       ferromagnetic
      </a>
      instruments.
     </p>
     <p>
      A specialized growing subset of interventional MRI is that of
      <a href="/wiki/Intraoperative_MRI" title="Intraoperative MRI">
       intraoperative MRI
      </a>
      in which the MRI is used in the surgical process. Some specialized MRI systems have been developed that allow imaging concurrent with the surgical procedure. More typical, however, is that the surgical procedure is temporarily interrupted so that MR images can be acquired to verify the success of the procedure or guide subsequent surgical work.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_resonance_guided_focused_ultrasound">
       Magnetic resonance guided focused ultrasound
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Magnetic resonance guided focused ultrasound">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRgFUS" title="MRgFUS">
       MRgFUS
      </a>
      therapy, ultrasound beams are focused on a tissueguided and controlled using MR thermal imagingand due to the significant energy deposition at the focus, temperature within the tissue rises to more than 65
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C2%B0C" title="C">
       C
      </a>
      (150F), completely destroying it. This technology can achieve precise
      <a href="/wiki/Ablation" title="Ablation">
       ablation
      </a>
      of diseased tissue. MR imaging provides a three-dimensional view of the target tissue, allowing for precise focusing of ultrasound energy. The MR imaging provides quantitative, real-time, thermal images of the treated area. This allows the physician to ensure that the temperature generated during each cycle of ultrasound energy is sufficient to cause thermal ablation within the desired tissue and if not, to adapt the parameters to ensure effective treatment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Multinuclear_imaging">
       Multinuclear imaging
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Multinuclear imaging">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Hydrogen is the most frequently imaged nucleus in MRI because it is present in biological tissues in great abundance, and because its high gyromagnetic ratio gives a strong signal. However, any nucleus with a net nuclear spin could potentially be imaged with MRI. Such nuclei include
      <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
       helium
      </a>
      -3,
      <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">
       lithium
      </a>
      -7,
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      -13,
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">
       fluorine
      </a>
      -19,
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-17" title="Oxygen-17">
       oxygen-17
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">
       sodium
      </a>
      -23,
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">
       phosphorus
      </a>
      -31 and
      <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">
       xenon
      </a>
      -129.
      <sup>
       23
      </sup>
      Na and
      <sup>
       31
      </sup>
      P are naturally abundant in the body, so can be imaged directly. Gaseous isotopes such as
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      He or
      <sup>
       129
      </sup>
      Xe must be
      <a href="/wiki/Hyperpolarization_(physics)" title="Hyperpolarization (physics)">
       hyperpolarized
      </a>
      and then inhaled as their nuclear density is too low to yield a useful signal under normal conditions.
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-17" title="Oxygen-17">
       <sup>
        17
       </sup>
       O
      </a>
      and
      <sup>
       19
      </sup>
      F can be administered in sufficient quantities in liquid form (e.g.
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-17" title="Oxygen-17">
       <sup>
        17
       </sup>
       O
      </a>
      -water) that hyperpolarization is not a necessity.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Moreover, the nucleus of any atom that has a net nuclear spin and that is bonded to a hydrogen atom could potentially be imaged via heteronuclear magnetization transfer MRI that would image the high-gyromagnetic-ratio hydrogen nucleus instead of the low-gyromagnetic-ratio nucleus that is bonded to the hydrogen atom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In principle, hetereonuclear magnetization transfer MRI could be used to detect the presence or absence of specific chemical bonds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Multinuclear imaging is primarily a research technique at present. However, potential applications include functional imaging and imaging of organs poorly seen on
      <sup>
       1
      </sup>
      H MRI (e.g., lungs and bones) or as alternative contrast agents. Inhaled hyperpolarized
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      He can be used to image the distribution of air spaces within the lungs. Injectable solutions containing
      <sup>
       13
      </sup>
      C or stabilized bubbles of hyperpolarized
      <sup>
       129
      </sup>
      Xe have been studied as contrast agents for angiography and perfusion imaging.
      <sup>
       31
      </sup>
      P can potentially provide information on bone density and structure, as well as functional imaging of the brain. Multinuclear imaging holds the potential to chart the distribution of lithium in the human brain, this element finding use as an important drug for those with conditions such as bipolar disorder.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_imaging_by_MRI">
       Molecular imaging by MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Molecular imaging by MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_imaging" title="Molecular imaging">
       Molecular imaging
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      MRI has the advantages of having very high spatial resolution and is very adept at morphological imaging and functional imaging. MRI does have several disadvantages though. First, MRI has a sensitivity of around 10
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Concentration#Molarity" title="Concentration">
       mol/L
      </a>
      to 10
      <sup>
       5
      </sup>
      mol/L which, compared to other types of imaging, can be very limiting. This problem stems from the fact that the difference between atoms in the high energy state and the low energy state is very small. For example, at 1.5
      <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
       teslas
      </a>
      , a typical field strength for clinical MRI, the difference between high and low energy states is approximately 9 molecules per 2 million. Improvements to increase MR sensitivity include increasing magnetic field strength, and
      <a href="/wiki/Hyperpolarization_(physics)" title="Hyperpolarization (physics)">
       hyperpolarization
      </a>
      via optical pumping or dynamic nuclear polarization. There are also a variety of signal amplification schemes based on chemical exchange that increase sensitivity.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      To achieve molecular imaging of disease biomarkers using MRI, targeted MRI contrast agents with high specificity and high relaxivity (sensitivity) are required. To date, many studies have been devoted to developing targeted-MRI contrast agents to achieve molecular imaging by MRI. Commonly, peptides, antibodies, or small ligands, and small protein domains, such as HER-2 affibodies, have been applied to achieve targeting. To enhance the sensitivity of the contrast agents, these targeting moieties are usually linked to high payload MRI contrast agents or MRI contrast agents with high relaxivities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A new class of gene targeting MR contrast agents (CA) has been introduced to show gene action of unique mRNA and gene transcription factor proteins.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17234603_139-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17234603-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24115049_140-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid24115049-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This new CA can trace cells with unique mRNA, microRNA and virus; tissue response to inflammation in living brains.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19295156_141-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid19295156-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The MR reports change in gene expression with positive correlation to TaqMan analysis, optical and electron microscopy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23150521_142-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid23150521-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_specialized_sequences">
       Other specialized sequences
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Other specialized sequences">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      New methods and variants of existing methods are often published when they are able to produce better results in specific fields. Examples of these recent improvements are
      <a href="/wiki/Relaxation_(NMR)" title="Relaxation (NMR)">
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        *
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       -weighted
      </a>
      turbo spin-echo (
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      TSE MRI), double inversion recovery MRI (DIR-MRI) or phase-sensitive inversion recovery MRI (PSIR-MRI), all of them able to improve imaging of brain lesions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another example is MP-RAGE (magnetization-prepared rapid acquisition with gradient echo),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which improves images of multiple sclerosis cortical lesions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetization_transfer_MRI">
       Magnetization transfer MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Magnetization transfer MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetization_transfer" title="Magnetization transfer">
       Magnetization transfer
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Magnetization transfer (MT) is a technique to enhance image contrast in certain applications of MRI.
     </p>
     <p>
      Bound
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       protons
      </a>
      are associated with
      <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
       proteins
      </a>
      and as they have a very short T2 decay they do not normally contribute to image contrast. However, because these protons have a broad resonance peak they can be excited by a radiofrequency pulse that has no effect on free protons. Their excitation increases image contrast by transfer of
      <a href="/wiki/Saturation_(magnetic)" title="Saturation (magnetic)">
       saturated
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">
       spins
      </a>
      from the bound pool into the free pool, thereby reducing the signal of free water. This homonuclear magnetization transfer provides an indirect measurement of
      <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">
       macromolecular
      </a>
      content in tissue. Implementation of homonuclear magnetization transfer involves choosing suitable frequency offsets and pulse shapes to saturate the bound spins sufficiently strongly, within the safety limits of
      <a href="/wiki/Specific_absorption_rate" title="Specific absorption rate">
       specific absorption rate
      </a>
      for MRI.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McRobbie_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-McRobbie-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The most common use of this technique is for suppression of background signal in time of flight MR angiography.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WheatonMiyazaki2012_147-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WheatonMiyazaki2012-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are also applications in neuroimaging particularly in the characterization of white matter lesions in
      <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">
       multiple sclerosis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Filippi2011_148-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Filippi2011-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="T1rho_MRI">
       T1rho MRI
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: T1rho MRI">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      T1 (T1rho): Molecules have a
      <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
       kinetic energy
      </a>
      that is a function of the temperature and is expressed as translational and rotational motions, and by collisions between molecules. The moving dipoles disturb the magnetic field but are often extremely rapid so that the average effect over a long time-scale may be zero. However, depending on the time-scale, the interactions between the dipoles do not always average away. At the slowest extreme the interaction time is effectively infinite and occurs where there are large, stationary field disturbances (e.g., a metallic implant). In this case the loss of coherence is described as a "static dephasing". T2* is a measure of the loss of coherence in an ensemble of spins that includes all interactions (including static dephasing). T2 is a measure of the loss of coherence that excludes static dephasing, using an RF pulse to reverse the slowest types of dipolar interaction. There is in fact a continuum of interaction time-scales in a given biological sample, and the properties of the refocusing RF pulse can be tuned to refocus more than just static dephasing. In general, the rate of decay of an ensemble of spins is a function of the interaction times and also the power of the RF pulse. This type of decay, occurring under the influence of RF, is known as T1. It is similar to T2 decay but with some slower dipolar interactions refocused, as well as static interactions, hence T1T2.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fluid_attenuated_inversion_recovery_.28FLAIR.29">
       Fluid attenuated inversion recovery (FLAIR)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Fluid attenuated inversion recovery (FLAIR)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluid_attenuated_inversion_recovery" title="Fluid attenuated inversion recovery">
       Fluid attenuated inversion recovery
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Fluid Attenuated Inversion Recovery (FLAIR)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      is an inversion-recovery pulse sequence used to nullify the signal from fluids. For example, it can be used in brain imaging to suppress cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) so as to bring out periventricular hyperintense lesions, such as multiple sclerosis (MS) plaques. By carefully choosing the inversion time TI (the time between the inversion and excitation pulses), the signal from any particular tissue can be suppressed.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Susceptibility_weighted_imaging_.28SWI.29">
       Susceptibility weighted imaging (SWI)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Susceptibility weighted imaging (SWI)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Susceptibility_weighted_imaging" title="Susceptibility weighted imaging">
       Susceptibility weighted imaging
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Susceptibility weighted imaging (SWI), is a new type of contrast in MRI different from spin density,
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <sub>
       1
      </sub>
      , or
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      imaging. This method exploits the susceptibility differences between tissues and uses a fully velocity compensated, three dimensional, RF spoiled, high-resolution, 3D gradient echo scan. This special data acquisition and image processing produces an enhanced contrast magnitude image very sensitive to venous blood,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemorrhage" title="Hemorrhage">
       hemorrhage
      </a>
      and iron storage. It is used to enhance the detection and diagnosis of tumors, vascular and neurovascular diseases (stroke and hemorrhage), multiple sclerosis,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
       <a href="#cite_note-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Alzheimer's, and also detects traumatic brain injuries that may not be diagnosed using other methods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neuromelanin_imaging">
       Neuromelanin imaging
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_resonance_imaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Neuromelanin imaging">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      This method exploits the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" title="Paramagnetic">
       paramagnetic
      </a>
      properties of
      <a href="/wiki/Neuromelanin" title="Neuromelanin">
       neuromelanin
      </a>
      and can be used to visualize the
      <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra" title="Substantia nigra">
       substantia nigra
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Locus_coeruleus" title="Locus coeruleus">
       locus coeruleus
      </a>
      . It is used to detect the
      <a href="/wiki/Atrophy" title="Atrophy">
       atrophy
      </a>
      of these nuclei in
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </a>
      and other
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinsonism" title="Parkinsonism">
       parkinsonisms
      </a>
      , and also detects signal intensity changes in
      <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">
       major depressive disorder
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">
       schizophrenia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
       <a href="#cite_note-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

       <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMagnetic+resonance+imaging&amp;rft.atitle=%2819%29F+MRI+for+quantitative+in+vivo+cell+tracking&amp;rft.aulast=Srinivas+M%2C+Heerschap+A%2C+Ahrens+ET%2C+Figdor+CG%2C+de+Vries+IJ&amp;rft.au=Srinivas+M%2C+Heerschap+A%2C+Ahrens+ET%2C+Figdor+CG%2C+de+Vries+IJ&amp;rft.date=July+2010&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC2902646&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.tibtech.2010.04.002&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmc%2F2902646&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F20427096&amp;rft.issue=7&amp;rft.jtitle=Trends+Biotechnol.&amp;rft.pages=363-70&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=28">
        <span style="display:none;">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.oxfordshireblueplaques.org.uk/plaques/mri.html" rel="nofollow">
        Blue Plaque commemorating the manufacture of the first commercial MRI whole body scanner at Osney Mead, Oxford
